I liked this movie a lot. Wonderfully performed and shot, fantastic score. But the story was outstanding too. It does slightly retrace Raiders and Crusade in structure, but that works so well for Indiana Jones. You establish him as an academic with genuine curiosity, if one uncomfortable with the daily work of being a professor, and then you pit him against literal Nazi's who are trying to literally weaponize artifacts from Jewish/Christian/Greek culture and mythology. That tension between a witty hero who really loves history versus villains who just want to use some founding myth of "The West" as a weapon will always be enough to sustain a couple of hours of chase scenes and banter. An excellent bow on this franchise.

Thank you for the first nuanced review I've seen for the film.  I was pleasantly surprised with Indy 5, maybe partly because of the exceedingly harsh reviews it has been getting.  I'm one of the rare Indy fans that doesn't completely hate Crystal Skull, but it's still my least favorite and that's mostly because of the overuse of (frequently bad) CGI that takes me out of the experience.  Dial is a major improvement in that regard, and most of the action feels somewhat realistic by comparison.  It was also a good idea to base most of the action around vehicles and horses, rather than fistfights and such, to make it a little more believable for an 80-year-old man.  The de-aging was a little distracting; they shouldn't have lingered so long in close-up shots, especially when he's talking.  I don't think the movie assassinated the character of Indy like some reviewers are arguing, and Waller-Bridge's character wasn't totally unbearable like I'd been led to believe either.  Considering all the circumstances this movie was made under (Ford's age, Spielberg and Lucas jumping ship, etc.), I thought this was about as good as an Indy sequel could be at this point in time.

Hey Chris, I actually liked the film. It is not on par with the original three. However, specific ideas and concepts in the movie were genius to me. As a history major who loves Greco-Roman history, I loved seeing the Antikythera as the main artifact. The actual Antikythera is evidence of the advancements conjured in the minds of the greatest intellects in the ancient world. It is, in essence, a computer. Many modern concepts come from the Greeks and Romans. However, following the Library of Alexandria's destruction and the Roman Empire's fall, many modern concepts were never implemented until the Renaissance rebirthed the ingenuity and desire for advancement that laid dormant during the dark ages. Seeing this in the film brought me the same joy as Indy when he saw Archimedes.     The Lance of Longinus (the Spear of Destiny) was also a good idea. Having it turned out a fake was even better because, in all honesty, the actual Lance, like most Catholic relics, including the Holy Grail, was fraudulent. Many of the holy relics worshiped by the Catholics during the Medieval times were put on exhibit by churches and cathedrals to get money from pilgrims on their way to Portugal heading west and the Holy Land heading east. The design of the cathedrals was that pilgrims (tourists) could see the relic without disturbing the mass services. The fact that churches and cathedrals all over Europe were miraculously able to acquire relics that were hundreds if not a thousand years old overnight raises questions about the relics' authenticity. The Lance of Longinus, which was supposedly acquired by many of the great conquerors of history, is likely a construct. As a Christian, I believe Jesus was pierced by an actual spear. However, that spear was lost or repurposed in the decades following the crucifixion. As an iron instrument, the soil and elements likely withered and destroyed its spearhead.

Couldn't agree more about the binary attitude in movie discourse: too often everything is treated as either amazing or terrible while, let's be honest, most movies usually range between okayish and pretty good. Really bad movies with nothing redeeming are rare and perfect movies are also very rare. Fortunately, there are many GREAT movies but they still have flaws and acknowledging them is okay and doesn't take away from how great they are. Likewise, there are many BAD movies but there's usually some aspect that deserves praise and it's frustrating when critics don't give credit to good stuff in bad movies. Nuance in criticism should be treated as something as important as nuance in art itself but it often isn't and it makes many reviews pretty frustrating to watch or read.

In Chris's review of Crystal Skull I was pleased to hear him lament the overexposed lighting of that film. To me, if I had to put my finger on it, that was the single biggest thing that made that film "feel" wrong. In hindsight, I can see what they were going for, trying to recreate the world of a 1950s Scifi B movie. The other main problem of that film for me was the supporting characters- poorly written, with weak motivations and just generally unlikeable. I was never especially bothered by some of the more controversial elements- the Nuke the fridge scene, and the sci fi ending- there were equally implausible things in all the previous films if one is being objective. What one can't deny, relooking at the Crystal Skull (the opening scene alone for example) is the sheer virtuosity of the filmmaking by Spielberg. The synergy between John William's score and the musical rhythm Spielberg's camera  capturing the musical beats is the equal of any of the first three films (perhaps even surpassing them at points). For me this latest film goes some way to capturing the aesthetic of the original films all-be-it some-what darker. On paper it is generally a better film, with stronger characters (for the most part) and more interesting ideas in terms of developing the character. But it still doesn't feel like an Indiana Jones film to me- because however competent a director James Mangold is- you just can't make up for the virtuosity of Spielberg- he does his best to try and frame it in a similar way, to move his camera in the same way, to use closeups in the same way- but for me, this film definitely lacks Spielberg's signature directorial touch.  This is most noticeable in the chases. Don't get me wrong, they are well done. But they just don't look like Spielberg. Nevertheless, I enjoyed it.

The era in which the original trilogy was made is part of the magic of what made those films great.   That's why KOTCS and DOD were never going to touch those original films.  Had they continued from Last Crusade every 3-4 years, THOSE sequels probably would have been considered classics like the other 3.  That's why these legacy sequels never hold up.   The filmmaking style and editing from that time period is what made those movies work, like lightning in a bottle.  Modern sequels to movies made in the 80's and 90's are never going feel right.   The best you can hope for is a touch of nostalgia and a couple of hours of entertainment.  It's perfectly fine to separate and enjoy them for what they are.  I liked Ghostbusters Afterlife a lot, but it did it recapture the magic of the first two?  Not even close.  But it didn't taint how much liked the originals.  I just enjoyed it for what it was.   I plan to enjoy Dial of Destiny the same way.

I'm not sure if it is a generational thing, but as a gen-xer (early, 1965) who grew up with such wildly varying effects in film and television in my childhood and adolescence that we learned to suspend disbelief in a radically different way than millennials and gen-z. We grew up watching analog, practical effects evolving and peaking with things like The Think and Alien, and then watching the total absolute beginning of digital effects starting with Tron, The Last Starfighter, and Willow and then viewing through the 90s being able to understand that the "realism" of an effect isn't as important as it's narrative purpose in the storytelling. The effects don't need to be realistic. We know it's a movie or tv show. We are find with what they need to do to tell the story they need to tell, which is why Rogue One's Grand Moff Tarkin doesn't bother me at all because it's an important narrative in the story and I don't care about how they used digital effects to recreate him. Same with digital de-aging. In the past they used practical makeup effects to age people up and down, and as a viewer, you just mentally 'get' what they are doing to tell the story. This is used in The Fellowship of the Ring with Bilbo when he finds the One Ring. We don't throw up our arms watching Fellowship and go "Welp! It's ruined! That's not a young Bilbo!" You accept things for the storytelling. Movies are like new memories you watch being formed before your eyes. Why do you need for it to be 100% realistic to accept it as part of the story being told? You really don't.

C'mon, man. Call this and The Crystal Skull what they were. With respect, this is, under the surface, whitewashing apologetics.  The first 3 films were... _fundamentally different._  They were each classics, even Temple of Doom, in its way.  These last two, could've been, sure, if they showed they knew what this whole Indiana Jones thing really always was...but they lost the road.   The cinematography, the ZERO-cgi action... and also, critically, the CHARACTER of indy, being so wry and trouble-prone and too curious for his own good... the aging Indy should be just as intense, but not FAKE him being so spry, rather brilliantly accommodating the action momentum around the PERSONALITY of his action movements... and exploit the aging to show beguiling similarities to his dad, and his own deepest questions and fears and motivations, now that he's immortal or whatever... There's so much they could draw on and from, that these could be new MODERN classics that are bursting at the seams with Indiana Jones character, charm, wit, personality, eternal adventuresomeness, hesitant cantankerousness, and all the REAL, thrilling live action cine-magic that it ever was... The 1940s adventure tropes were off-the-charts - but recapturable, if truly honored, with a glint in one's eye.  I mean, this epic saga is some of the most thrilling, compelling adventure cinema of all time!  The bar, I'd say, by which anything similar must be held in some kind of perspective.

I think there is an inherent problem with the new Indiana Jones movies.  Imagine the old, original three movies, being shot with a 75 year old main character. That wouldn't have worked, right? Indy was a man in his prime - young, attractive, risk-taking and brash. The original movies would probably not have been a success with your young self, had they cast someone the age of your grand-dad as their lead.  There is an obvious solution to this problem though - and a path to that solution has been laid out in The Last Crusade. Sean Connery as Henry Jones Sr. was an amazing addition to the series, and the father-son chemistry added a lot to the fun. And there were hints that Henry Jones Sr. in his younger years might have been just as impetuous - and that that's the main reason why he is so derisive of his sons impetuousness in The Last Crusade. But even with Sean Connery bringing his a-game, the clear and obvious lead character was Indy. Indy was the one the action revolved around, and Connery was often just sitting/standing there, expecting his son to do all the dirty/risky stuff.  And that's where The Crystal Skull failed. They had the right idea with introducing Indy's son Mutt - but they made him into a secondary character, more of a side-kick rather, and still put an mostly unchanged grandpa Jones into the center of all the action. Now Shia LaBeouf's performance in that movie was anything but stellar - and you can blame his acting or the direction if you want - but I think there's no way he ever could have succeeded, as long as Ford remains the central lead character.  What would have been needed is for old Indy to evolve as a character - and to become more like his own father. Dropping the nickname "Indy", becoming "Henry Jones Sr.", and growing calmer, more measured and less brash. And make him the supporting character, while Mutt takes his father's mantle and becomes the new Indiana Jones - and the center of the action.  And that's something Hollywood would never dare to do these days. Evolving and changing a beloved character is always going to be divisive, and in the age of internet outrage, can be dangerous to success. And that's why Indiana Jones hasn't grown as a character - but still is exactly the same adventurous daredevil as he was in his - now long faded - youth.  But while this made his younger self charming and roguishly attractive - it makes 75 year old Indy feel like someone who is desperately clinging to his long-gone youth, because he's never learned or grown beyond that.  But instead of introducing a new, young Indy - what they are doing is de-aging Ford... *sigh*
I was looking forward zu Indy 5 for many years. I was optimistic when Mangold was announced as a director. But Disney has done so many shitty things recently, I can't give them my money anymore without feeling disgusted afterwards. So no, I won't buy a ticket for Indy 5 just to watch an okay movie. If it would be as good as many of Mangold's strongest works, I'd make an exception. But sadly it isn't.   Greetings from Germany :=)

Here's my problem with the "binary thinking" argument here, and why it's not exactly acceptable for this movie to "just be okay": ....WHY is it "just okay"? As more people get a chance to weigh in on media, the comments that rise to eye level will be less verbally sophisticated and less wordsmithy - but they aren't less fundamental or less resonant. I'd bet that for all Ebert's sophistication and beautiful articulation, his reviews wouldn't land with a real sample of every north-american the same way a clever tweet or meme would now. The cost of democratizing social participation is that the median expression isn't that of an artist, but of an everyday-person just bein' themselves. But! Don't mistake that for social decline. It's a fundamental change in who gets a say in favour of what actually resonates with most people.

The biggest issue with the Indiana Jones franchise post-Last Crusade is CGI. And that goes for the majority of big Hollywood blockbusters. I'm not saying CGI is bad- it can be done well and tastefully. But there's too much of a reliance on it to where suspension of disbelief is thrown completely out the window. And it goes all the way back to the writing- if "anything can be done" on film, then anything and everything is thrown into the script without a second thought of how they'll pull it off.

My Favorite Movie Franchises Ever 1. Back To The Future 2. Indiana Jones 3. Marvel Cinematic Universe 4. Star Wars 5. The Godfather üêê 6. X-Men 7. Batman 8. Spider-Man  9. Mission: Impossible  10. James Bond  Honorable mentions The Lord of the Rings (TRoP ruined it)  Monster-Verse Predator Bill & Ted Harold & Kumar  Dumb & Dumber Beavis & Butt-Head  Austin Powers  Naked Gun  Wayne's World  Cheech & Chong   I'm aware of some major omissions like Star Trek and Harry Potter. Or the oldest one, Godzilla. Or even Die Hard, Lethal Weapon, Alien, Toy Story, or other animated franchises like Shrek, The Incredibles, and Kung-Fu Panda. I've never seen all of them or any of the old Superman movies with Christopher Reeve. I actually have all these movies stored in my 2 TB external hard drive but never have time to watch them.   I think MCU Phase 3 from 2016-2019 with 11 films might be the greatest run any franchise ever had. MCU's batting average was off the charts with the only stinker to me being Captain Marvel. It had heavyweights like Civil War, Infinity War, and Endgame. Then two Spider-Man films, Doctor Strange, GotG Vol. 2, Ragnarok, Black Panther, and Ant-Man and the Wasp.   Then Phase 4 ruined it. The decline in quality was drastic. I'm talking a freefall. I know it's hard to write great movies or TV shows for a long time. The quality will eventually degrade as time goes by. We can't expect it to be always great forever. But man, what a freefall! From Endgame to twerking in She-Hulk?   Ones that have consistently disappointed -  Terminator (ü§¶üèª‚Äç‚ôÇÔ∏è after T2)  Transformers Pirates of the Carribean Fast & Furious (I only like 5 & 7)  I'm not into horror films but my previous girlfriends were into them and I have seen a few Friday the 13th and Nightmare on Elm Street films. Nuance doesn't seem to want to exist in today's society, much less the (relatively) simple act of film criticism. When every Tom, Dick, and Harry has access to put their uncultured opinion about a movie into the public square, the educated, savvy, and knowledgeability of all-time greats like Siskel & Ebert get ignorantly dismissed, lost, or outright forgotten. That's today's challenge with giving everyone the mic to give their opinion on everything: idiotic, nonsensical, and uneducated takes get mixed in with the educated ones, essentially muddying up the whole profession. I was a fan of both franchises by 1989-1990 or several years before I got into Star Wars in early 1997 when the SEs came out. I even love their rides in Universal Studios (Hollywood) and Disneyland. I'm aware BTTF the Ride is gone but I thought it was a blast. The last time I rode the Indiana Jones ride in Disneyland in 2010 (or was it in 2005?), it was such a blast..   I would buy the DVD box sets for The Godfather, Back To The Future, Indiana Jones, and Star Wars on release days at my closest Best Buy from 2001-2004. All four trilogies (at the time) were Day 1 purchases for me. BTTF and Indiana Jones were always my favorite because I never felt they needed a fourth film. They didn't ruin their brand with countless sequels that Star Wars eventually fell victim to like the Fast films.   I can't see Indiana Jones be portrayed by anybody else except Harrison Ford. Yeah, yeah, I know River Phoenix portrayed a young Indy in The Last Crusade. And multiple ones (mainly Sean Patrick Flanery) portrayed him in The Young Indiana Jones Chronicles. But I can't see Chris Pratt portray Indy. It needs to be Harrison even if he's 80. It feels right like it felt right seeing Keaton donning the Batman suit even with wrinkles on his old face.  I know Crystal Skull gets a lot of hate. And yeah, I can completely understand. When I first saw it in the theater in 2008, I was underwhelmed.  My buddy from Colorado told me in early-2009 it was a piece of shit. But something happened around 2010. My stepdad would get burned copies of a bunch of movies from a family friend for free. I remember watching Crystal Skull on loop every night for months in 2010. It actually became a film I grew to love even with so many stupid scenes like the giant ants and Tarzan swinging on vines scene with Shia.  I really didn't hate Crystal Skull except the last 10 minutes of it. I didn't mind John Hurt's character. The 1950s stuff was cool. The greaser stuff was cool. I actually found the nuclear scene getting inside a refrigerator quite fascinating. Shia wasn't terrible in it and I like how he handled a switchblade without needing a coach. Cate Blanchett was having fun in her role. The only annoying character was Mac. Crystal Skull is a bad movie that I grew to love. Dial of Destiny could end up the same. Maybe that first viewing won't be so great. It's with repeat viewings that it gets better.   So in my eyes, there isn't an Indiana Jones film that I've seen that I hated yet. I enjoyed them all. High replay value. A charismatic hero. Great locations. Legendary theme music. Fun sidekicks. I love the Uncharted games but Nathan Drake is no Indiana Jones to me. Neither is Lara Croft. So if Kristian Harloff is saying Dial of Destiny is better than Kingdom of the Crystal Skull, then it's already a W in my book.

This video gets so unbearably pretentious at 4:09 that it's hard to take the rest of the video seriously.   It seems that Chris doesn't understand how Roger Ebert became popular in the first place. It wasn't because he offered nuanced analysis. He plunged into the sensationalism of film discussion with his overdramatic movie reviews. Most people tuned in to his reviews back when they were being aired on TV because they wanted to hear him trash and lambast a movie.   People have always responded to negativity.   Most people loved his trademark binary rating system (thumbs up = good and thumbs down = bad). He focused more on expressing his emotional reaction to the movies he watched rather than on insightful analysis, because he was more interested in not spoiling movies rather than in-depth film criticism.   Chris, if you read some of Roger Ebert's more academic style essays on film analysis, then sure, okay, I can believe that you'd find that he's probably more intelligent than today's film journalists, but Chris, don't lie to yourself, there's no way you got that impression through his reviews, because Roger Ebert wrote reviews for the common folk, and that's what made him popular. I can't believe that you claim to not have let your romanticized feelings about Indiana Jones get in the way of your review of the movie, when you can't even recognize how you've romanticized Roger Ebert's career.   Chris, you don't know what you're talking about, so keep Roger Ebert's name out of your mouth.

Your review is touching, credible, intelligent, sensible, balanced and of a professionalism which, as usual, honors you. I'm sure the great Roger Ebert would look at you with the eyes of a spiritual father. All of your allusions to Maestro John Williams hit the emotional target every time (is there a single soul who doesn't appreciate the works of John Williams?). As for the quality of the special effects, I believe that the advent of computer-based digital effects is to be blamed. Since ''Terminator 2'' basically, we are conditioned to expect perfection. The slightest anomaly will tickle our attention. I grew up in the seventies and eighties. We were much more tolerant back then because we knew the limits and appreciated the effort and passion put into it. We could see that it was stop-motion, matte painting, puppets, etc... but we thought it was cool anyway and we appreciated the resemblance. Computer effects have greatly helped cinema but have also dehumanized it in a certain sense. I will always prefer the Yoda puppet, articulated and animated by Frank Oz, more than the digitized version, as realistic as it might be. Just like I prefer reading a real book than its digitized version. Can we today watch a film filled with special effects and simply appreciate the effort without focusing on the slightest anomaly? As for the character of Phoebe Waller-Bridge, I have not yet seen the film but, of course, what I hear does not delight me. I absolutely hate anything that is, directly or indirectly, Woke, falsely progressive and exaggeratedly feminist. And believe me, I LOVE Sarah Connor, Leia, Gal Gadot's Wonder Woman, Ripley, Rey, Marion and the Halliwell witch sisters! I admire Michelle Obama and I love the tenacity of Tulsi Gabbard, for example. All this to say that when feminism and progressivism is logical, healthy and honest, it is a plus for films and humanity (Audrey Hepburn, Cate Blanchett, Kate Winslet...). But when it's forced, indoctrinated and politicized, it's not for the good of movies and humanity at all! It destroys and divides (Brie Larson, Kathleen Kennedy and, apparently, Phoebe Waller-Bridge). That's what Disney and Marvel have been doing for two, three years. They rot everything they touch. This is what I read and hear about Indy 5 and Phoebe Waller-Bridge. She is not there for the sake of the script but to indoctrinate. Even some frecnh newspapers have said it. This is another female character from Disney who, rather than complementing the male hero, crushes him, renders him useless, stupid and relegates him to the background. That's not what Sarah Connor, Leia, Lois Lane or Marion were doing with Kyle Reese, Han Solo, Superman or Indy. That's not even what Wonder Woman is doing with Steve Tremor. After seeing the film, if I was completely wrong, I will be the first to admit it and praise the character of Phoebe Waller-Bridge. More power to her and good for all of us! But in light of what I hear from Kathleen Kennedy and Ms. Waller-Bridge themselves, unfortunately, I don't think I'm all that wrong. Anyways, we'll see. If you read this far, thank you. If you didn't, well... God bless you anyway.

4:09- 4:53 blame the grifters online who make film discourse toxic in fandoms specifically (you know who I'm talking about)
The problem with de-aging, and this applies to CGI VFX in general, is that anything put in a computer model does not move as we would expect it to move in real life. Not even mocap solved that problem fully. It does not bother us when it is aliens or the Na'vi from Avatar because out eyes don't have a real-life baseline. However, when you put a young Harrison Ford on screen, our subconscious automatically kicks in, looking for facial queues and body movements, which are missing. You can build a 100% accurate image of Indy in a computer, but the minute it moves, our brains say "No."

Film criticism and film culture online is at its very worst. It's dire. Chris being so level-headed here is so refreshing. Because he's right. Everything is extremes now. A film, especially an IP based film, either has to BLOW YOUR MIND OMG BEST THING EVER or else it's the worst piece of shit to ever exist. So much of what the entertainment culture is now is so antithetical to actually being able to just sit down, watch a film, and make up your mind about it as a work unto itself. It all comes with so much fucking baggage now.   Just focusing on big IP-based sequels and reboots like Indy 5...it seems that there is a built in expectation fans simply won't/can't recognize - that these movies somehow NEED to magically tap into the magic you felt at 10 years old seeing for them first time. Sorry, that's will NOT EVER HAPPEN. The original three films had multiple decades to tattoo themselves into our psyches - to cement themselves into the zeitgeist. How can any late-stage sequel compete with that?   Fandoms lack any sort of normal perspective. Its insane. Everyone is jaded. Everyone is cynical. Everyone has arbitrary criteria for what these kinds of films NEED to be. I hate it. Talking about movies online is almost a fools errand now. Even though I'm not religious, I always found Indy was at his best dealing with questions of faith with SOME supernatural elements but also fighting actual human villains, not fanciful ones. Temple of Doom and Crystal Skull both got away from that and just had me rolling my eyes - or cringing at its depictions of other cultures.¬†  I believe Steven Spielberg directed all  3 of the original movies, and if he's not back for this, I don't know how good it's going to be. Crystal Skull had way too much CG as well, and one of the things that made Indy great was all the practical stunts and effects that were shot for real. They still can't duplicate that in CG. I'm not against it to remove wires for stunt doubles, but I shouldn't be questioning if what I'm looking at is legitimate. Even Terminator 2 knew that and that's why it's a great film. Not everything was CG, so the CG still looks believable to this day.   Spielberg has shown that he knows how to make an action movie that also tells a story and stays focused on that story. But if you get another director trying to replicate what Spielberg did in the 80s, with much older actors, it becomes a bit like watching a Rolling Stones concert in 2023. Some of the magic gets lost. I like that, at least from the trailers, Karen Allen and John Rhys Davies are both back.¬†  But again, if these are just going to be fantasy movies rather than grounded action films with some elements of fantasy and the supernatural, I don't know how interested I am. Maybe I'll see it if it's released in 3D. The last thing I want to watch is something that comes off like A View To A Kill, with Roger Moore at almost 60 years old trying to be a convincing spy while doing things no man of 57 would be able to do at all. I want to remember Indiana Jones for Raiders and Last Crusade, where everything looked real and I could suspend my disbelief somewhat. You never want to see your favourite band from 20 years ago show up at an award show to perform and all you're thinking is just how old and ridiculous everyone looks trying to act like they did as teenagers and young adults while 40 year olds pretend they're 16 again. That's just cringeworthy.
